Reasons and circumstances to allow ‘requests for returns’ and ‘reversals’
While most types of Faster Payments transactions are final and irrevocable once the payment is settled,
financial institutions may submit requests for returns and reversals in case the transactions are deemed
fraudulent or due to error.
FedNow

FedNow’s credit transfers can be returned either when a FedNow participant decides to honor a return request
(camt.056) message or when the FedNow participant cannot apply the funds it received, for example, when
rejecting a payment after initially responding with an ‘accept without posting’ status. Once a payment return
(pacs.004) is initiated the message follows the same end-to-end processing across the FedNow Service as a
customer credit transfer (pacs.008). For that reason, the return request (camt.056) message also can be used to
report fraud. Similarly, a liquidity management transfer (pacs.009) can be returned if a participant determines
it is necessary.
RTP

The sending FI in the RTP Network may initiate a Request for Return of Funds if it encounters errors
in the payment, claims unauthorized transactions, or experiences processing issues leading to incorrect
payments. These include unauthorized RTP payments, erroneous RTP payments, or RTP payments made
in response to fraudulent payment requests. A sender or sending FI issuing a Request for Return bears no
liability to the receiving participant, as it does not constitute the cancellation of an RTP payment. The
receiving participant is not obliged to return funds in these cases. All credit transfers are finalized upon the
system’s processing of the positive response message from the receiver FI. On the other hand, a Request for
Return of Funds serves as an appeal to the receiver FI to investigate the possibility of fund retrieval, with
no guarantee of successful recovery.
Same Day ACH

The ODFI in Same Day ACH can rectify an incorrect entry or file by sending a reversing entry to the RDFI.
This addresses entries transmitted erroneously or without the proper authorization of the originator. When
the sending ODFI has made processing errors it can request the RDFI to return an entry. These instances
include duplications, incorrect payment orders, variations in payment amounts, unauthorized credit entries
(e.g., corporate account takeover), and discrepancies in the timing of debit or credit entries. However, relying
on reversals does not guarantee a successful correction of errors, as they may be rejected due to reasons such as
insufficient funds or other valid causes. The decision to return an entry lies at the discretion of the RDFI. File
reversals can be initiated to fix issues like duplicated files or files with a significant number of incorrect entries.
It is the responsibility of the party that originated the duplicate or erroneous file to reverse that file. A failure
by the ODFI to fund a credit file is not considered a valid reason for initiating a file reversal. Each reversing file
must be in the format required by ACH specifications.
Push-to-card Network

For push-to-card networks the sending FI must reverse the original pull funds transactions when payment
transactions fail for reasons that can include a lack of funds. For Visa Direct the original pull funds transaction
can be reversed using the Adjustment API, which initiates a financial message called an Account Funding
Transaction Reversal (AFTR). The ReverseFunds operation can be processed for a single account or multiple
accounts simultaneously. A reversal of an originating credit transaction can be processed only when the reasons
for the reversal include incorrect payment credentials, incorrect transaction amount, duplicate processing, or
incorrect transaction code.

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

85

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Timeframes for processing ‘requests for returns’
When it comes to submitting requests for returns, each payment service operator requires a specific timeframe for
senders and sending FIs to follow. The FedNow Service strongly recommends that receiver FIs respond as soon
as possible, but no later than midnight of the next standard business day. Only FIs that decide to investigate for
future information can take up to ten standard business days. The RTP Network also indicates that receiver FIs
should respond within ten standard business days of receiving the request for return. When there is a need for
further investigation of the claimed fraud or breach of warranty, FIs can take longer than ten days.
For Same Day ACH every reversing entry should be sent to the ACH Operator within a timeframe that
ensures it reaches or is accessible to the RDFI within five banking days from the settlement date of the
erroneous entry. Both the sender and ODFI are required to transmit each reversing file promptly and,
when applicable, respond to a correcting file, to the ACH Operator within five banking days following the
settlement date.
When returning funding transactions, Mastercard Send requires that funding reversals be submitted within
30 minutes of the funding transfer request. For Visa Direct, a reversal of an Original Credit Transaction
(OCT) must be processed within one business day of the processing date of the transaction.
TABLE 42. FASTER PAYMENTS NETWORK –
TIME FRAMES FOR PROCESSING ‘REQUESTS FOR RETURNS’
FASTER PAYMENTS NETWORK

TIME FRAME

FedNow

Receiver FI should respond to the return request as soon as possible, but no later than
midnight of the next standard business day.

RTP Network

Receiver FI should respond within 10 banking days of receiving the request for the return
of funds, however may take longer than 10 banking days to investigate the claimed fraud or
breach of warranty.

Same Day ACH

Sending FI/ODFI must transmit the file to the ACH Operator within 24 hours of the
discovery of the error, and Receiver FI/RDFI should receive the reversal file within 5
banking days after the settlement date.

Mastercard Send

Receiver FI must submit funding reversals within 30 minutes of the funding transfer request.

Visa Direct

OCT reversals must be processed within one business day of the processing date of the
transaction.

Post-transaction exception evaluation
Faster Payments utilize various methods to create, record, and retain information needed to evaluate issues that
may arise after a payment has been initiated. These methods can involve monitoring, payment tracing, incident
tracking, and using transaction IDs, though specific procedures for handling such transaction exceptions can vary
depending on the Faster Payments rail.
FedNow

All return request (camt.056) messages contain a unique message identification, assigner, assignee, and
creation date time. The case is assigned a unique identification and the creator of the investigation case
when the investigation of the request begins. This can include contact details in case further discussion
of the request or a request response is needed. Return request messages must also indicate a reference
to the original customer credit transfer. This information comprises of original message identification,
original message name identification, original creation date time, original instruction ID, original end-toend ID, original interbank settlement amount and settlement date, etc., to help the other counterparty
identify the transaction requested to be returned. Cancellation reason information is required, which
specifies the reason for the return request and should contain a code from the ISO 20022 externalized

86

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

CancellationReason1Code list. Reason code FRAD, for instance, is be used to report fraud and meets the
FedNow Service fraud reporting requirement.
RTP Network

A Request for Return of Funds message is initiated by the sending FI to the receiving FI to request the
receiving FI investigate and return funds for any reason. The Response to the Request for Return of Funds
message may be a positive response, indicating that funds will be returned, or negative (rejected), indicating
that funds will not be returned. In either case, the response is not expected to be immediate, but after an
investigation by the sending FI. The sending FI may returns funds either via the Real-time Payment System
or another Payment System. If sending via the RTP Network, the receiving FI will create a new Credit
Transfer message (pacs.008) that includes the original transaction’s unique ID to the corresponding Request
for Return of Funds in the Credit Transfer (pacs.008).
Same Day ACH

For Same Day ACH reversals it is necessary that the word “REVERSAL” is placed in the Company Entry
Description Field of each Company/Batch Header Record. In addition, ODFIs must ensure that the sender
or ODFI’s name reflects the RDFI identified in the erroneous entry being reversed when initiating a reversal.
The ODFI of the reversing entry may make negligible variations to the original content of the Company
Name field, such as for accounting or tracking purposes, on condition that the name of the sender remains
readily recognizable to the receiver. Same Day ACH further recommends that ODFIs set a reasonable level of
monitoring and tracking parameters that look out for questionable transactions that are classified Code R17
(File Record Edit Criteria/Entry with Invalid Account Number Initiated Under Questionable Circumstances).
Mastercard Send

Mastercard manages and records the information needed for post-transaction exception evaluation by
employing transaction identifier codes to track payments. These codes serve as unique identifiers, ensuring a
connection between various authorization requests and the corresponding transaction. Transaction identifier
codes for Mastercard require the following identifying data elements: transaction date, a Trace ID code, a
BankNet reference number, and the date of the payment settlement. The unique identifier generated becomes
a critical part of the transaction authorization and clearing process and is used in tracking payments as a part
of exception processing.
Visa Direct

Visa Direct utilizes the status of Direct API calls which include various error codes, HTTP status code
returned in the response, and other information in the response message to process exception handling. Any
reverse funds request generally requires data elements such as the Systems Trace Audit Number, Retrieval
Reference Number, Transaction Identifier, approval Code, Date and Time of Transmission, and Acquiring
BIN, as well as sender’s account information.

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Knowledge of the types and purposes of back-office systems and
functions for processing payments
Faster Payments rails’ impact on OFAC screening processes
The adoption of Faster Payments Systems in the U.S. have presented new challenges to sanctions screening
because of the shorter processing times. OFAC sanctions screening ensures that transaction parties are not on
the U.S. Treasury Department’s denied party list for international or domestic transactions. OFAC sanctions
screening is conducted by FIs and third-party service providers via software. Faster Payments systems require
processes to be completely automated so as to minimize the risk that funds are sent to a sanctioned entity. This
in turn requires that FIs subscribe or otherwise access sanctions screening lists on a 24/7/365 basis rather than
download batch-based files that can be checked prior to payment message submission.
To address this challenge, the Office of Foreign Assets Control has introduced a risk-based advisory approach
for sanctions screening of Faster Payments. Compliance with OFAC regulations must focus on management
commitment, risk assessment, internal controls, testing, auditing, and staff training. OFAC recommends
developers integrate sanctions screening compliance into the design of Faster Payments systems by facilitating
communication between FIs, exception processing, and establishing minimum compliance expectations for
participants. Faster Payments systems rely on FIs to verify customers against the OFAC sanctions list during
onboarding and at regular intervals, although screening is usually conducted on an individual basis for
international payments.
Faster Payments rails’ impact on AML compliance processes
All FIs are required to enact policies geared towards identifying and preventing instances of money laundering.
Legal requirements for AML efforts springs from several pieces of legislation, including the Bank Secrecy Act
of 1970 (requiring FIs to assist government agencies in the detection and prevention of money laundering), the
USA PATRIOT Act of 2001 (which enhanced these provisions and required more advanced record keeping and
reporting), and the Money Laundering Control Act of 1986 (which made money laundering a federal crime).
FI AML efforts include the establishment of AML policies, generalized sanctions screening, monitoring,
and reporting unusually large Currency Transaction Records (CTR) (daily aggregate totals over $10,000) or
Suspicious Activity Reports (SAR). Suspicious transactions could include frequent structuring of transactions
to evade reporting requirements, abrupt changes in usual account activity, rapid movements of funds, or
international transactions to high-risk jurisdictions. The limits for SAR are generally lower vary by state. FIs
achieve compliance via monitoring by properly trained staff or machine learning-powered data analysis.
However, SLAs around processing and settlement speed can hinder non-automated transaction analysis and
FIs’ ability of to conduct AML efforts for Faster Payments systems. Common responses to this problem have
included the usage of advanced data analysis, such as artificial intelligence and/or machine learning, to
quickly identify potential cases of money laundering as well as to identify and release false positive cases. FIs
and operators can also increase training and staff size for the review and resolution of flagged transactions,
though the 24/7/365 nature of many Faster Payments mean that some form of automation is required.
All Faster Payments systems require participant compliance with AML legislation, customer identification
measures, and periodic due diligence checks against sanctions lists. Faster Payments systems also require
participant FIs to have sufficient AML policies, programs, and units in place. Obligations around AML
compliance also require these systems to account for the usage of third-party senders and verify their AML
compliance. The card networks require participants to comply with network-specific AML programs which
include standard monitoring and due diligence checks, and mandatory responses to periodic AML assessments
and questionnaires.

88

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Faster Payments rails’ impact on fraud detection processes
The speed and irrevocability of Faster Payments systems drastically shrinks the time period in which stakeholders
are able to spot and identify fraud, presenting opportunities for fraudsters. Fraud detection strategies for Faster
Payments require more modernized approaches than traditional payment systems, and a variety of different
fraud mitigation strategies are used.
‘Smart friction’ is often used in Faster Payments to help with fraud mitigation by creating artificial friction
that forces users to think twice before following through on a payment initiation. Examples of this include
confirmation-of-payee, in which the sending user is notified that the name on the beneficiary account does
or does not match the name input by the sender, indicating the potential for fraud or a misdirected payment.
Multi-factor authentication is another example.
Community-wide information sharing is another anti-fraud tool used in Faster Payments systems. Communitywide information-sharing involves system participants sharing fraud-related intelligence and information
among each other; sometimes this sharing is done on a voluntary basis and sometimes on a mandatory basis.
This can take many forms, including flagging accounts known to be used by fraudsters, alerting participating
FIs of suspicious transactions or types of transactions, or forms of centralized reporting.
End user education measures also further strengthen fraud mitigation practices for Faster Payments systems
by providing more information on the differences between individual payment rails and what this means in
terms of liability recovery for funds. This information empowers users to make more informed choices, adopt
more secure practices, and recognize potential red flags on fraud. This is important because consumer liability
differs significantly between the different types of Faster Payments, something end users may not be aware of.
Additionally, the various Faster Payments systems employ VAS and anti-fraud tools to combat the threat posed
by fraudsters. For the FedNow service, fraud prevention tactics including the use of participants-defined negative
lists (akin to a suspicious entities list), mandatory fraud reporting measures, digital signatures for all transactions,
and the use of data encryption. For the RTP Network, TCH requires the usage of MFA authentication for all
payments. Fraud screening can be conducted by FIs using software to identify unusually high-value or frequent
transactions, as well as those sent to unlikely beneficiaries for this purpose. For Same Day ACH payments
services are offered aimed at fraud detection and OFAC screening, along with proxy identification services.
Both MasterCard and Visa offer fraud detection solutions that utilize advanced data analysis and machine
learning to identify potentially fraudulent transactions.
Faster Payments rails’ impact on deposit systems
Financial institutions offering Faster Payments face challenges related to the speed and duration of payment
processing, i.e., instead of having a cycle-based business day, posting to beneficiary accounts are now 24/7/365.
Batch payments work in cycles, with specific windows for different steps of the processing flow, such as, collecting
payment requests, file creation, validation, submission, clearing, settlement, and posting. However, faster
payments require all these steps to happen within minutes – if not seconds. One consequence of this is
that deposit systems must be able to move funds from settlement accounts to their customers’ accounts
much faster, requiring financial institutions to plan their liquidity needs in advance rather than being able
to adjust them to the batch payments value at hand. Automation and updating business processes are key
to handling these challenges as are having sufficient technical backups, enabling 24/7/365 operations and
system maintenance.
While this is less of an issue with Same Day ACH, FedNow, RTP, Mastercard Send and Visa Direct all come
with these challenges, though push-to-card is less intense than with the instant systems.

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

89

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Reporting requirements necessary to process Faster Payments
Providing faster payments requires increased planning, and thus reporting from FIs, to limit risks arising due to
the speed of payments. Internal reporting capabilities have to be tuned to prepare FIs to the uncertainty related
to Faster Payments. Firstly, regarding the instant systems, banks must ensure sufficient liquidity 24/7/365 because
they do not have time to adjust liquidity. This is less of an issue with push-to-card. Second, payments processing
system failures are greatly damaging when it comes to Faster Payments because FIs have a much shorter buffer
window: with the instant systems there is no buffer. Third, monitoring systems need to be enhanced so as to
process AML, CTF, OFAC, and fraud checks all within a tight deadline for push-to-card and instant systems.
Back-office systems’ controls for Faster Payments processing
Financial institutions need a number of back-office systems to support the seamless processing of Faster
Payments. First, liquidity management systems are responsible for guaranteeing that an FI has sufficient
liquidity at its disposal to handle all expected transfers. Fraud management systems help prevent unauthorized
transactions from being initiated, thereby protecting customers from losing funds due to fraud. Third, sanction
and OFAC clearing systems make sure that transactions initiated do not involve any individual or business that is
on any sanction list.
Back-office systems needed to meet audit and reconciliation requirements
Participants of Faster Payments rails are expected to utilize internal reporting for improvement and are subject
to external audits. This means FIs need to operate certain back-office systems that facilitate reporting. The audits
are usually concerned with compliance with system rules, payments reconciliation, downtime, and fraud. Hence,
back-office infrastructures ought to include systems to match transaction records with reports provided by the
system operators, systems that collect data of liquidity usage and forecasts future needs, systems that keep track
on fraud-related events and those that keep track of availability on payment rails, prevent connection failures and
calculate adequate planned downtime to sort out arising system issues.

Knowledge of the interoperability considerations regarding foreign and
domestic payment systems
Interoperability across Faster Payments rails
Interoperability between Faster Payments systems is rare in the US. Aside from the two push-to-card networks,
which are able to send payment messages between one another, none of the other systems are interoperable.
The ACH Network can carry some ISO 20022 data, but does not have the infrastructure to enable the
instant transfer of message by message routing. As for the ISO 20022 based solutions, at this time they have
adopted slight variants of the standard and cannot be exchanged directly. At the time of this writing, there
have been no initiatives by developers to enable any greater interoperability between systems, directly.

Having similar messaging formats makes it much easier for payment systems to be interoperable, either in
the domestic case or internationally. Without this, each system has to decode and translate messages from
other systems to their own standards, which can lead to data truncation and fragmentation issues, which is
inefficient in terms of time and cost.
A common messaging standard is beneficial and promotes greater interoperability, easing cross-border
payments and enabling a consistent service delivery for end users. SWIFT developed ISO 20022 to create a
single standardized approach for all payment systems and their participants. ISO 20022 is an open standard
providing a common language for multiple types of transactions, providing richer and more structured data
compared to proprietary standards. Despite ISO 20022’s introduction, there are differences between markets
in terms of implementation, which creates inconsistences.
While only FedNow and RTP in the U.S. use ISO messaging standards to send payments, card networks
also utilize APIs to enable participants to initiate transactions. Some participants might find APIs more
beneficial than ISO connections as it is to implement and use. Furthermore, Mastercard and Visa have
incredible reach, so joining their networks via API is the easiest way for FIs to connect with thousands of
other institutions around the world.
Risk factors related to cross-border payments
Financial institutions are faced with a multitude of risk factors that must be managed when processing
cross-border payments. These risks encompass a broad spectrum ranging from regulatory compliance risk
(AML, CTF, and sanctions screening), correspondent relationships, currency fluctuations, liquidity, and
general payment infrastructure stability. The stability of general payment infrastructure depends on a
country’s physical and technological conditions. A notable instance is the recent service disruption in
the Japanese national clearing system. In this case, inadequate risk management by the clearing network
resulted in a failure of backup measures, causing a two-day delay in all payments both domestic and
cross-border. Managing these risks is critical to ensuring institutional integrity, transaction fulfillment,
legal compliance, and business risk.

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

91

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Screening compliance risks

FIs facilitating cross-border transactions must ensure compliance with pertinent regulatory considerations,
especially sanctions screening, CTF, and AML concerns. These regulations ensure that FIs are not a party
to terrorism financing or money laundering activities. Compliance with varying regulations across different
jurisdictions poses significant challenges, as anti-terror financing legislation varies across jurisdictions. KYC,
transaction monitoring, and monitoring correspondents’ due-diligence procedures can also be insufficient
or not up to US standards, making it difficult to know the ultimate beneficiary of a cross-border transaction
or identify suspicious transaction patterns. Adding in the time pressures inherent to Faster Payments also
means that these processes need to be done quickly, which presents its own challenges.
Foreign exchange & liquidity risks

Currency fluctuations and liquidity issues present a risk factor to financial institutions for cross-border
payments because currency fluctuations impact profitability and create operational challenges, especially
when time changes and operating hours of FX markets are taken into account. Unpredictable exchange rates
can complicate financial planning and risk management, which in turn can lead to losses and operational
inefficiencies for FIs. Liquidity in these currencies also needs to be managed. Currency fluctuations can also
increase settlement risk, as FIs can end up needing to settle for larger amounts than accounted for in liquidity
management programs.
Infrastructure stability risks

Infrastructure stability can significantly impact cross-border payments. The resilience and reliability of the
technological and logistical systems that underpin payment systems are paramount to the fulfillment of
cross-border payments. Disruptions to supporting infrastructure in the form of natural disasters, cyber-attacks,
or system failures can lead to transaction delays, unmet SLAs, financial losses, and even compromised data
security. Examples of this would include Japan’s national clearing system experiencing delays due to failures in
backup measures, showcasing the vulnerability of the nation’s payment infrastructure. Similarly, disruptions in
the UK’s RTGS system CHAPS in 2023 resulted in temporary payment (domestic and cross-border) delays,
emphasizing global repercussions when critical payment infrastructure faces instability. Examples such as
this demonstrate the importance of robust risk management and resilient financial infrastructure for the
functioning of cross-border payments.
Barriers to cross-border interoperability of Faster Payments rails
Cross-border interoperability for Faster Payments rails face numerous challenges, including differing payment
messaging standards, AML and sanctions screening, foreign exchange issues, settlement methods, and other
factors. Of the Faster Payments systems covered in this handbook, only the push-to-card networks currently
allow for cross-border payments, though the RTP Network is working on a cross-border system with the
Eurozone instant system RT1.
Barriers to cross-border payments
Screening procedure barriers

AML and sanctions screening represent obstacles to cross-border interoperability of Faster Payments systems.
This is because receiving institutions have to trust that screening and onboarding procedures in other markets are
accurate and meet the same requirements as in the home market. Furthermore, the nature of Faster Payments
means that time to verify transactions is limited, meaning that automation is key as well as a source of risk. A lack
of universal AML and sanctions compliance requirements across jurisdictions, varying regulatory approaches

92

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

and onboarding requirements, and fears of reputational/financial loss by FIs lead to significant risk in the
processing of cross-border payments.
This is one reason why the correspondent-respondent relationship has been so successful – trust can be
difficult to establish and is hard to do on a market-wide basis.
Fragmentation in International Data Standards

International data standards present significant barriers to cross-border faster payments, primarily arising from
fragmentation and uncertainty within data frameworks. One significant challenge lies in the inconsistency across
jurisdictions regarding the implementation of rules and standards. This fragmentation extends to jurisdictionspecific requirements for messaging formats and legal documentation, resulting in manual interventions and
higher rates of payment rejections. Moreover, payment service providers grapple with balancing various
obligations under different data frameworks, such as regulatory and supervisory requirements, third-party
provider usage, and data security and privacy mandates. The uncertainty surrounding these obligations
hinders efficient cross-border Faster Payments transactions and raises concerns about compliance with
regulations like anti-money laundering and combating the financing of terrorism (AML/CFT).
Additionally, restrictions on data flow across borders adds on to the existing challenges. Mandates for
data localization impose significant costs on FIs in terms of overhead and maintenance, impeding their
ability to identify fraud, comply with regulations, and manage risks effectively. These measures not only
hinder innovation within the Faster Payments system but also degrade data security and system resilience.
Innovations that could enhance payment efficiency, like full payment traceability and prepayment validation,
are stifled by the lack of a cohesive global approach to data standards. Consequently, these barriers not only
impede the optimization of cross-border Faster Payments systems but also limit competition and innovation,
ultimately affecting the accessibility and cost-effectiveness of such transactions.
Foreign exchange barriers

Foreign exchange can present obstacles to cross-border instant payments due to currency conversion
costs, transparency concerns, tight SLAs, fluctuations in exchange rates, and FX market operating hours,
among other factors. These factors collectively increase the cost, uncertainty, and risk associated with
these transactions as well as potentially harming the end user experience. Currency conversions can
introduce hurdles to payment fulfillment due to the additional transaction costs involved, fluctuating
exchange rates, and desire to conclude relationships with FX service providers. Furthermore, restricted
operating hours for FX markets contribute an additional obstacle, as transactions may be delayed or
affected by the limited availability of trading opportunities. The costs involved with these payments
can also be uncertain and untransparent, creating ambiguities for the FIs involved as well as end users.
Stipulations around SLAs can be difficult to maintain, increasing the risk of timeouts, while also making
AML, CTF, and sanctions screening more difficult, increasing legal risk.
Settlement barriers

Settlement for Faster Payments can present special issues depending on the type of Faster Payments system being
used. While instant settlement systems, such as FedNow and RTP, are more likely to use a form of synchronized
settlement – whereby transactions settle in both currencies synchronously – this is purely theoretical as neither
system is live with cross-border solutions. Push-to-card systems do offer cross-border payments and are therefore
the only Faster Payments system to do so. Each transaction settles, but typically does so after posting, meaning
that net positions and currency issues are solved after the transaction posts to the beneficiary’s account. Same Day
ACH is not available for cross-border at all.

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

93

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Messaging standard barriers

Messaging standards are an issue for cross-border interoperability. ISO 20022 in its current application represents
a common base for formatting payment messages, but it is not used by all Faster Payments systems, neither in the
U.S. nor abroad. Furthermore, its use varies consistently across different markets even that do use it for domestic
payments, which can lead to confusion and friction in cross-border payments as well as data loss and
misinterpretation of messages. This can, in turn, lead to payments not being accepted, being wrongfully
flagged by AML or sanctions screening software, and lead to additional work in the form of exceptions
handling on both sides of the transaction. Information in ISO 20022 messages can be structured differently across
markets. For example, TaxIDs in Germany, Social Security Numbers in the US, and Aadhaar numbers in India serve
the same purpose but their use in ISO messages can vary, complicating the translation of messages.
Solutions to cross-border interoperability for Faster Payments rails
This section explores key avenues to alleviate friction and complexities associated with cross-border payments,
focusing on screening procedure harmonization, the establishment of cross-border system links, foreign
exchange friction reduction, and messaging standard harmonization. By delving into these potential (and
mostly unrealized) solutions, this section aims to illuminate how frictions to cross-border payments can best
be navigated.
Screening procedure harmonization

Harmonizing sanctions screening lists and AML procedures, as a part of more generalized information-sharing
and standardization program, presents a major challenge and opportunity to cross-border interoperability. A
more unified approach to sanctions screening lists and AML protocols could streamline compliance efforts across
financial markets. If FIs were able to adhere to more uniform AML procedures and screening lists, discrepancies
could be reduced and a more cohesive approach to risk management of cross-border payments could be
cultivated. Collaborative efforts among regulatory bodies could also involve heightened information sharing
among FIs and regulatory bodies, which could reduce friction and cultivate a more streamlined cross-border
payment experience.
Establishing cross-border system links

One solution to settlement issues for cross-border transactions is the establishment of bilateral systems. This
can best be exemplified by initiatives like UPI establishing settlement links with countries like Singapore and
the United Arab Emirates, or the pilot program IXB, which links TCH RTP to EBA Clearing’s RT1 system
in Europe via synchronized settlement. Bilateral systems offer a targeted solution to alleviate settlementrelated frictions in cross-border payment interoperability. By establishing direct connections between systems,
these bilateral arrangements bypass intermediary channels, reducing delays and potential errors that tend to
be prevalent in cross-border payments facilitated by correspondent banks. This approach enhances the speed
and efficiency of cross-border settlements. Transactions can be executed more directly between participating
FIs, leading to a more seamless and interoperable international payment landscape. The downside is the sheer
number of connections needed given the number of currencies around the world.

94

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Foreign exchange

One way to reduce friction involved in foreign exchange (FX) for cross-border payments is through innovative
measures such as implementing a centralized module listing FX prices of various financial institutions for specific
currency corridors. This centralized platform would provide transparency and real- time information on exchange
rates, minimizing uncertainties, enable price shopping, and reduce negotiation hurdles. Alternatively, the
introduction of a centralized body to facilitate FX transactions could streamline processes, offering a standardized framework. Introducing a centralized body to offer foreign currency exchange would cut down on
friction associated with converting payments into different currencies. However, potential trade-offs, such
as concerns about price gouging by a potentially monopolistic entity, would have to be carefully considered.
Messaging standard harmonization

Standardizing the usage of ISO messaging standards and harmonizing sanction screening lists and procedures
can offer a robust solution to alleviate challenges in the fulfillment of cross-border payments. Entities like the
Committee on Payments and Market Infrastructure (CPMI) have developed voluntary harmonization guidelines
set to be adopted by 2027. These guidelines encompass recommendations on messaging types, standardized
time conventions, transparent currency conversions, and globally accepted payment codes. Harmonization,
specifically for ISO 20022, has the potential to mitigate challenges in cross-border payments arising from
different usage of data formats leading to loss of data or incorrect data placement and interpretation This
harmonization enhances information transparency, enabling operators to swiftly analyze crucial components
of international payment messages and streamline transaction processing.

Knowledge of the types of user transparency and remittance
information provided by Faster Payments rails’ stakeholders
Faster Payments rails facilitate the transmission of both value and non-value information through messages.
Participants and the system can inquire as to and provide information on the status of each payment message,
ensuring that participants remain informed at every step of the process flow and can take appropriate actions.
Besides the information contained within the payment messages themselves, participants in Faster Payments rails
utilizing the ISO 20022 data standard can share extensive remittance information. This facilitates multiple
business processes, benefiting participants and both the sending and receiving users.
This section provides an overview of the types and functions of status messages within Faster Payments rails
and outlines how remittance information is utilized and shared within each payment network.
Types and functions of status messages
FedNow and RTP – Types and functions of status messages

Messages exchanged between participants, and the Faster Payments system
Both participants and the system itself use messages to share the status of payment messages, return messages,
share additional information about the payment, and share a participant’s connection status. The table below
shows the type of messages available to FedNow and RTP participants.

Same Day ACH – Types and functions of status messages
Messages exchanged between participants and the Faster Payments system
In the ACH Network ODFIs can learn the status of a payment (i.e, whether it was rejected). They must notify
the ODFI the reason for the rejection if the message was rejected by the RDFI or the ACH Operator. There are
defined reason codes for each reason. Other types of messages notify participants of the status of a payment, such
as payment acknowledgment messages. These non-monetary entries are sent to acknowledge a corporate credit
or debit (CCD) payment entry.

after receipt of the file from the ODFI. The status information indicates whether or not the files have
successfully passed editing and validation routines. Acknowledgment is in the form of a message or report
made available to the ODFI.

In the case of Mastercard Send the receiving FI needs to approve the transaction, which is then shared with
the sending FI through the Mastercard Network. Participants of both Mastercard Send and Visa Direct can
obtain information about the transaction by sending a request message via API.

Remittance information
Remittance information refers to the supplementary details or data associated with a payment, offering
additional insights into the payment’s purpose, the payment receiver, etc. Remittance information can be
transmitted together with the payment or in a separate non-value message. This information can improve
payment reconciliation processes for businesses and individuals, fostering transparency and streamlining
financial processes.
RTP – Remittance information

The RTP Network allows the sending user to send unlimited characters via extended remittance information
about the payment to the receiving user in addition to credit transfer messages. The sending FI may send a
standalone message called a remittance advice (ISO 20022 message ID remt.001) message to the receiving FI
if the information does not fit into the limited remittance fields within the credit transfer message. A remittance
advice message may also be sent in conjunction with an RFP as an invoice.
•

The sending FI ensures that the remittance advice message properly references the credit transfer
message by including the credit transfer’s unique ID.

•

The receiving user may send a message confirming receipt of funds and provide additional
information. For example, the payment acknowledgment by the receiver (ISO 20022 message ID
camt.035) message may include a note or information from the receiving user such as:
-

“Thank you for your payment. Goods have shipped with tracking number: 123456789.”

-

“Your payment has been received and credited to your account. Your available credit following
payment is $5,000.00.”

FedNow – Remittance information

When it comes to FedNow, participants may provide optional remittance information within customer credit
transfer messages and RFP messages via a short free-form text field or detailed with designated elements, such
as specific fields for an invoice number, the amount due, due date, and others. FedNow also supports reference
remittance information that is made available separately, e.g., including a hyperlink to a document posted on
a web portal.
Same Day ACH – Remittance information

In the ACH Network an ACH addenda record is an optional record attached to an ACH payment carrying
remittance information. It can be described as a note or a memo attached to the payment that’s used to

98

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

provide supplemental information needed to identify the account holder or to provide additional payment
information to the receiving user/business.
As an example, Corporate Credit or Debit (CCD) and Corporate Trade Exchange (CTX) entries are
distinguished by the ability of the sending and receiving users to exchange various amounts of remittance
information. Companies that need to transmit remittance data with the CCD entry may add an addenda
record to carry the payment related information (CCD+ transactions), such as invoice and order numbers.
The CTX format supports the transfer of extensive addenda records, up to a maximum of 9,999 records
each carrying 80 characters of payment-related data, along with the transfer of the payment. The CTX
format is typically used to pay multiple invoices that are listed in the addenda records, although it may be
used for a single invoice too.
Addenda records may use the American National Standards Institute (ANSI) Accredited Standards
Committee (ASC) X12 transaction sets and data segments to relay payment-related information. However,
CTX transactions may also include ISO 20022 remittance advice messages (remt.01 and remt.02) if both
the sending and receiving FIs have opted-in to participate in Nacha’s XML-ACH Remittance Information
Opt-in Program.
Push to card – Remittance information

Visa Direct and Mastercard Send messages are processed with a different structure than A2A Faster
Payments rails. The message standard and the required fields to process a payment vary from those used by
FedNow and RTP, where ISO 20022 is used.
Remittance information in push-to-card networks is additional information carried with the payment such
as the sender reference, the sender identification number for businesses, etc. Both networks determine the
type of data fields and information that should or must be included and the information that may optionally
be included. Both networks enable VAS APIs that help participants obtain additional information about the
payment.
Remittance information disclosure requirements
It is expected that participants pass along the remittance information to receiving users. However, Faster
Payments networks usually do not determine how this information is passed along. This decision is left
to participants. This section describes the requirements that Faster Payments networks have (if any) with
regard to disclosing remittance information.
ACH Network – Remittance information disclosure requirements

In the context of the ACH Network (particularly relevant to CCD and CTX entries carrying addenda
records), the Nacha Operating Rules require RDFIs provide all payment-related information contained
within the addenda records transmitted with CCD and CTX entries upon the request of the receiving
user (in this case a business). RDFIs must provide the receiving user with the requested information by
the opening of business on the second banking day following the settlement date of the entry to which the
payment-related information relates. Nacha Operating Rules don’t dictate how this information is shared
with the receiver (e.g., in human-readable or machine-readable format).
Other networks do not provide guidelines.

Knowledge of the payment network process flows by participation level
Payment process flows
The payment process flow is fairly similar for all Faster Payments systems available in the United States. They
include five stakeholders: the payer, the originating institution, the payment system, the receiving institution
and the payee; and four steps: payment initiation, submission, routing and posting. While the number of
stakeholders can expand when technical service providers, initiation service providers, and settlement agents
are used, these processes were simplified for the sake of clarity. Regarding the four steps involved, these, too,
were simplified to only took at the payment flow rather than all of the potential checks and potential errors
that could occur along the way.
Figure 7. Generic payment process flow

One can differentiate between the type of third parties involved in the payment processes. Same Day ACH
and instant payment systems have the same type of third parties that help participants mainly with banking
operations, corresponding banking, and settlement.
Figure 8. Payment process flow for instant payments and Same Day ACH

Card networks allow participation for third parties that provide switching services to support payment processing
in addition to ones that facilitate payment initiation between payers and originating financial institutions.

Differences in clearing process

Clearing describes what happens to a payment message between initiation and the settlement of obligations
arising from a payment transaction. This usually includes the transmitting and exchanging of data and
reconciling and confirming of transfers in addition to fraud and sanction screening.
The speed of Faster Payments shortens the clearing periods compared to traditional payment methods, as
instant payments and card networks must post funds in a matter of minutes or even seconds. These systems
have integrated clearing systems that validate payment messages based on their compliance with operating
rules and formatting standards, accept or reject payment messages from originating financial institutions
and route eligible payment requests to the receiving institutions. Due to their settlement timeframes card
networks have additional time to facilitate data exchange and clarify inconsistencies between participants,
which can be up to 30 minutes.
Same Day ACH has a longer clearing window (though shorter than traditional ACH). This means that
there is more time to for ODFIs to run checks on payment messages. Payments rejected by either the RDFI
or the receiving operator can be recreated and resubmitted.

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Knowledge of the availability, permissible maintenance windows, and
outage notifications by rail
Every participant must ensure they meet the system’s SLAs as per participation requirements. Faster Payments
rails acknowledge participants’ needs to conduct system maintenance that may impact availability. The systems
also recognize the possibility of unforeseen failures affecting system connections. Each Faster Payments rail
determines the procedures to follow in cases of system maintenance, outages, and the implications for message
transactions through its operational and technical guidelines. This section provides details on how each of
these topics is addressed for the various Faster Payments rails.
Availability expectations for accessing Faster Payments rails
Faster Payments rails in some cases are available 24/7/365, though there are some exceptions. For example, some
networks allow for short non-connectivity periods (permissible downtime) or make the 24/7/365 availability
requirement for receiving messages but not necessarily for sending messages (e.g., participants can set their own
sending windows). The ACH Network is open for processing payments 23¼ hours every business day (i.e.,,
participants can submit payments to the Network within these operating hours). However, payments are
only cleared and settled during Same Day ACH cycles. Moreover, Same Day ACH payments can only be
settled when the Federal Reserve’s settlement service is open (currently, the Federal Reserve’s settlement
system is closed on federal holidays and weekends, and business days from 6:30 p.m. ET to 7:30 a.m. ET).
System maintenance and outage notification requirements
Faster Payments rails acknowledge that participants may require planned downtime to conduct system
maintenance. This section covers how, when, and for how long participants should carry out planned system
maintenance for each Faster Payments rail as well as how the network and participants should be notified
of unplanned outages.
TABLE 51. SYSTEM MAINTENANCE REQUIREMENTS BY FASTER PAYMENTS RAILS
SYSTEM MAINTENANCE REQUIREMENTS PER FASTER PAYMENTS RAILS
FedNow

Participants planned downtime should not exceed two sequential hours or 24 hours total per quarter, and any planned
downtime must be scheduled on Sundays between 2 a.m. and 6 a.m. ET. Participants and Service Providers are
expected to take steps to reduce their need for planned downtime that better accommodates continuous operations
over time.

RTP Network

Participants must satisfy a standard of 99.5% continuous connectivity to RTP measured on a calendar month basis,
excluding permissible downtime; and be available to accept or reject messages while maintaining this connectivity
standard. The permissible maintenance window is each Sunday from 2:00 am to 6:00 am (ET)

Same Day ACH

There are no specific requirements. Participants typically conduct system maintenance when the processing windows
are closed.

TABLE 52. OUTAGE NOTIFICATION REQUIREMENTS BY FASTER PAYMENTS RAILS
OUTAGE NOTIFICATION REQUIREMENTS PER FASTER PAYMENTS RAILS
FedNow

Participants should contact the FRB Services Support Center for guidance on unplanned downtime and any action to
be taken. Participants are also recommended to sign off from the system so that other participants are informed
(FedNow notifies other participants through a broadcast message). It is also recommended that participants
implement internal processes to disable sending messages during the unplanned downtime.

RTP

Participants must send a participant sign-off (ISO 20022 message ID = admn.003) message to RTP.

Same Day ACH

There are no specific requirements

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Straight-Through Processing expectations of Faster Payments rails and implications of
transaction rejects (e.g. transaction timeouts)
Straight-through processing (STP) refers to when a payment process is completely electronic and automated
from beginning to end. STP helps reduce the time and cost of processing transactions, minimizing the risk of
errors. This is particularly relevant in the context of Faster Payments due to expectations of immediate funds
delivery. The higher the Straight-Through Processing (STP) rates, the faster payments can be processed endto-end and delivered to the receiving user as defined in the network’s SLAs.
One crucial aspect of meeting system SLAs is to prevent transaction timeouts. FedNow has established a
timeout clock that sets expectations for processing speed and capability for both the sending and receiving
FIs. The timeout clock begins when the payment message is forwarded by the sending financial institution
to the network and has a duration of 20 seconds and ceases counting just before settlement occurs. This
means that receiving FIs can determine the number of seconds needed to be reserved for them based on
the time it takes for them to process payments. A receiving FI can reserve up to five seconds of the clock,
ensuring this time is guaranteed as part of the flow. The timeout clock will expire prematurely if, based on
the configuration set by the receiving FI, there is insufficient time remaining for the receiving FI to provide
a response (i.e., process the payment).
Faster Payments processing results in a higher rate of successful transactions, as the receiving FI reserves
fewer seconds, allowing more time for the transaction to be processed end-to-end.
TABLE 53. TRANSACTION TIMEOUT BY FASTER PAYMENTS RAIL
TIMEOUT PERIOD BY FASTER PAYMENTS NETWORK
FedNow

Timeout clock expires after 20 seconds

RTP

Messages time-out at 15 seconds, starting from when the message reaches RTP for processing

Mastercard Send

For a Payment Transfer API, if Mastercard Send does not receive a response from the receive network within 40
seconds a timeout will occur

Visa Direct

Timeout will occur in 30 seconds if the API is not able to complete the transaction

Knowledge of the procedures for reporting fraudulent and/or
suspicious activity related to Faster Payments
Fraud classifications for Faster Payments
Faster Payments present a unique fraud risk due to the speed in which the transaction is processed and the
subsequent reduced timeline for fraud checks. Fraud can be classified into two main forms, authorized or
unauthorized, and often involves sophisticated techniques such as identity theft, account takeover, and/
or phishing. The unique challenge for Faster Payments is to develop robust anti-fraud practices without
compromising the speed and convenience that these payments provide. Addressing fraud in Faster Payments
therefore requires a multidimensional approach to mitigation.
The Federal Reserve published its FraudClassifier as a tool to enable FIs to better understand how fraud
occurs in the payments industry. The FraudClassifier allows FIs to classify fraud independently of payment
type, channel, or characteristics.
The FraudClassifier utilizes several parameters, the first of which is whether the fraud was authorized (in
which the account owner authorizes fraudulent transactions due to deceptive tactics) or unauthorized (in
which the transaction transpires without the account owner’s knowledge or approval). Authorized fraud is
further divided into whether the party was manipulated, acted fraudulently, or whether an unauthorized

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

103

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

party manipulated the payment information. Unauthorized fraud is where the unauthorized party took over
the victim’s account or the victim’s payment information was misused.
Figure 10. Federal Reserve FraudClassifier

Fraud reporting obligations
FedNow

FedNow participants are required to report to the central service when fraudulent transactions occur.
However, the particulars of FedNow fraud reporting requirements are confidential.
Same Day ACH

Consumers are required to notify their financial institutions within 60 days of when fraud occurs, though
businesses only have 24 hours. Regulation E requires that the fraud must be reported to the ODFI or the
defrauded party to be compensated. FIs are required to investigate instances of fraud and report it to the
relevant authorities, with overall responsibility lying with the ODFI.
RTP

Fraud reporting by RTP participants is mandatory under the RTP Technical Specifications and Risk
Management/Fraud Controls requirements. Financial institutions must notify both the counter-party FI
and The Clearing House via the RTP Network as soon as possible when fraud occurs or is suspected.
According to the RTP Operating Rules participants must also act promptly based on any alerts of fraud or
suspected fraud.

104

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

Mastercard Send

Mastercard has established clear reporting requirements in cases of fraud for stakeholders involved. FIs are
obligated to promptly report fraudulent activity to the relevant authorities as well as to Mastercard’s Fraud
and Loss Database. APIs are also available to enable real-time reporting of fraud for participant entities in
the Mastercard scheme.
Visa Direct

Any entity within the Visa payment value chain that suspects or confirms fraud has taken place is required to
comply with network reporting requirements. FIs are required to report instances of fraud to Visa within 3
business days and subsequently must submit an incident report to Visa following an independent investigation.
All other relevant parties involved with the fraudulent or suspected fraudulent transaction must also be notified.
Visa’s Risk Investigations group must also be provided with all account data pertaining to the transaction, after
which Visa may conduct its investigation.

Methods and messages used for reporting fraud
Each Faster Payments network utilizes its own methods and message types for communicating cases of fraud
to both the system itself and other participants. Some networks have specific message types or reason codes
according to the type of fraud (e.g., authorized or unauthorized), while other networks notify cases of fraud
more generally.
The information below summarizes the messages used for reporting fraud per network.
FedNow and RTP – Methods and messages used for reporting fraud

•

Fraud reporting with “Return request (camt.056)”: The return request message camt.056 is used to
report fraud along with a request to returning funds from a previously sent message. For example, if a
participant sends an instant payment and later identifies it as having been fraud, the participant sends
a camt.056 including the reason code “FRAD” to notify the receiving FI.

•

According to RTP rules the FRAD code cannot be used in a request for Return of Funds message
(camt.056) unless the sending FI has determined that a payment is an unauthorized payment. For
payments that a sender claims were made under false pretenses (e.g., authorized fraud), the “CUST”
code must be used.

Same Day ACH – Methods and messages used for reporting fraud

Participants must use the appropriate reason code to reflect the reason for payment return/rejection. The
information below describes the reason codes used in cases of fraud or suspected fraud.
•

R03 “No Account/Unable to Locate Account”: This reason code is used when the account number
structure is valid and it passes the check digit validation, but the account number does not correspond
to the individual identified in the entry or the account number designated is not an existing account
(e.g., reflecting a case of potential authorized fraud).

•

R06 “Returned per ODFI’s Request”: This reason code is used when the sending FI has requested
that the receiving FI returns an erroneous entry, or a credit entry originated without the authorization
of the sending user (e.g., potentially reflecting unauthorized fraud or error)

•

R10 “Customer Advises Originator is Not Known to Receiver and/ or Originator is Not Authorized by
Receiver to Debit Receiver’s Account”: This reason code is used when the receiving FI has been notified

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

105

C H A P T ER TWO – FASTER PAYMENTS OP ER ATION S AND P R O C E S S FLOWS

by the receiving user that it does not know the identity of the message originator, has no relationship
with the message originator, or has not authorized the originator to debit his or her account.
•

R17 “File Record Edit Criteria/Entry with Invalid Account Number Initiated Under Questionable
Circumstances/Return of Improperly-Initiated Reversal”: This reason code is used when field(s)
cannot be processed by the receiving FI, the entry contains an invalid account number e.g., account
closed/no account/unable to locate account/invalid account number and is believed by the receiving
FI to have been initiated under questionable circumstances, or the receiving FI or the receiving
user has identified a reversing entry as one that was improperly initiated by the originating user or
originating FI.

A practical example of the reason code R17 can be described as follows: A sending user (in this case a business/
company) receives an R17 return that includes ‘QUESTIONABLE’ in the addenda information. The payment
is being returned for more than an account number error and it is possible that the company may be a victim
of fraud. An R17 in conjunction with this description will enable these returns to be differentiated from other
returns with more routine account number errors and flag them for closer examination.
Push-to-card – Methods and messages used for reporting fraud

Push-to-card networks provide participants with error codes when transactions fail so that participants react
to transaction errors, including those errors related to fraud.
Mastercard Send
When a sending FI sends a payment transfer request to the network (through an API call), the sending participant
receives an API response indicating whether the transaction was approved or declined (i.e.,, those that resulted
in a 402 error response). Error response messages may contain specific Error Detail Codes. The code 130002 is
used when the transaction was declined because fraud was detected. Participants can obtain further information
(i.e., the network response code) on declined transactions using GET requests. The ISO Code 83 is used when
the transaction was declined for fraud/security reasons.
Visa Direct
Similar to Mastercard Send, participants use the combination of the HTTP status code returned in the
response, error code from Visa Direct or the platform, and other information included in the response
message to determine the status of any Visa Direct API call. These error codes and response messages
indicate the nature of the declined transaction, including in cases of suspected fraud.

C H A P T ER THR EE – FASTER PAYMENTS RISK MAN AG EMEN T

Chapter Three

Faster Payments Risk
Management
SECTION BLUEPRINT COMPOSITION: 19%
Types of risks present in Faster Payments
The high velocity of payments handled by Faster Payment systems may lead to a number of risks for
stakeholders. As users increasingly adopt Faster Payments, understanding and mitigating risk grows in
importance. These risks, if not addressed, can affect not only the smooth functioning of the payment system,
but also the stability and trust in Faster Payments themselves. The upcoming sections will elaborate on several
risks that Faster Payments face, including operational, cross-channel, compliance, fraud, liquidity, technology,
and credit risks.
Operational risks present in Faster Payments
Operational risk represents the amount of loss related to unintentional errors, which may result in delayed,
duplicated, or otherwise erroneous payments. All financial market infrastructures face operational risk that
originate due to deficiencies in information systems or internal processes, human error, management failures,
or disruptions from external events that result in the reduction, deterioration, or breakdown of services.
Operational risk poses a huge challenge particularly in many Faster Payments systems because of the need for
continuous system availability 24/7/365. This imposes significant demands on operational reliability for Faster
Payments and participants. Speed, on the other hand, magnifies the impact of any operational incidents leading
to delays or interruptions in Faster Payment services, and these interruptions are immediately noticeable to end
users. The repercussions of an operational incident in Faster Payments may manifest much sooner in comparison
to traditional retail payments, where service interruptions or slowdowns could go unnoticed.
A Faster Payment system being unavailable for a few minutes can cause many payments to fail; the consequences
of operational downtime can therefore have even more serious implications than with traditional systems. As
users become accustomed to utilizing Faster Payments for time-sensitive payments, any interruptions could affect
users and result in penalties for late payments or insufficient funds for other transactions.
Faster payment systems are subject to fraud and security risks, forming a large subset of operational risks.
These risks can originate from internal or external sources and are closely linked to each other.
Cross-channel risks present in Faster Payments
Cross-channel risk occurs when the movement of fraudulent or illegal payment transactions from one payments
channel to another is met with inconsistent risk management practices and lack of information sharing across
THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

107

C H A P T ER THR EE – FASTER PAYMENTS RISK MAN AG EMEN T

payment channels about fraud. A financial institution offering faster payment products in addition to traditional
payment products (e.g., checks, wire transfers) is exposed to increased cross-channel risk due to the multiple
payment channels available.
The speed and finality of faster payments reduce the time available for fraud detection and prevention.
This can make it easier for fraudsters to potentially move funds quickly through different channels before an
institution can review and detect suspicious activity.
Compliance risks present in Faster Payments
Compliance risks in Faster Payment systems pose a significant challenge for FIs and other stakeholders
involved in processing these payments. The major factor contributing to this increase in compliance risks
arises from the increased speed of most types of Faster Payments. Concerns around insufficient time to
adhere to regulatory guidance, anti-money laundering (AML) protocols, KYC due diligence, and fraud
prevention are increasingly being solved by automation, which leads to its own problems. The high velocity
of Faster Payments provides a smaller window of opportunity for stakeholders to conduct thorough due
diligence and real-time monitoring, potentially increasing the risk of non-compliance and fraud.
FIs must navigate the delicate balance between meeting account holder expectations and satisfying regulatory
requirements around financial crime and risk controls. This poses a considerable challenge for FIs who are
accustomed to lengthier time periods with the possibility of manual intervention. Despite the challenges
associated with compliance, the cost of non-compliance can be very steep. Penalties for non-compliance can
include fines, suspension, and even exit of the FI from the Faster Payment systems.
Fraud risks present in Faster Payments
Fraud risk is the risk that a payment transaction will be initiated or altered in an attempt to misdirect or
misappropriate funds. Risks related to fraud are affected by internal as well as external factors. Fraudulent
activities may be the work of dissatisfied, disgruntled, or dishonest employees, third-party processing personnel,
originating company personnel, or other outside parties (e.g., cyber thieves, fraudsters). Some common fraud
scenarios in Faster Payment include:
•

End user manipulation by a fraudster, resulting in the issuance of a payment instruction bound for
the fraudster by the payer acting in good faith (i.e., credit-push fraud)

•

Payment initiation by a fraudster that has fraudulently obtained the payer or payee’s sensitive
payment data

•

The modification of a payment attribute (such as the account number, transaction amount, or name
of the payee or payer) of a genuine payment instruction intercepted by the fraudster

Fraud risk has the potential to affect all actors in the payment value chain, including end users, FIs, and the overall
Faster Payment system, and these risks are common across both Faster Payments and traditional retail payments.
As with operational risks, the end-to-end speed of Faster Payments, especially the immediate availability of funds,
makes Faster Payments more lucrative and susceptible to fraud risk than traditional payments.
The Federal Reserve’s FraudClassifierSM model provides some guidance on how to systematically classify
fraud based on three key questions:
•

Whether an authorized or an unauthorized party initiated the payment

•

How the fraud was executed

•

What tactic was used to commit fraud

108

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

C H A P T ER THR EE – FASTER PAYMENTS RISK MAN AG EMEN T

Using this model and adding payment attributes (such as payment amount, date, type, and channel) to these
classifications can provide additional insights to help aid the organization in identifying fraud trends.
Liquidity risks present in Faster Payments
Liquidity risk is the current and potential risk to earnings or capital arising from a financial institution’s inability
to meet its obligations when they come due. That is, liquidity risk occurs when a financial institution cannot settle
an obligation for full value when it is due even if it may be able to settle at a future time.
Liquidity risks in Faster Payment systems depend on the type of settlement model being used by the system. For
systems such as FedNow and RTP, which utilize real-time settlement, liquidity risk is minimal because settlement is
pre-funded and each transaction is settled in real time during payment processing. For push- to-card systems such
as Mastercard Send and Visa Direct, which use prefunded settlement, even though the settlement happens same
day or next day, the liquidity risk is mitigated as the funds are blocked for settlement at the time of processing.
However, Same Day ACH FI relies on deferred net settlement so there is liquidity risk that builds up between
the settlement cycles. Liquidity is primarily required at the end of each settlement cycle, introducing potential
risks if inter-PSP settlement (Inter-PSP settlement refers to the process of settling funds between Payment Service
Providers (PSPs) after a transaction has occurred between their respective customers. This is a critical component
of fast payment systems and cross-border transactions.) occurs beyond normal business hours. To mitigate such
risks, tools like prefunding, liquidity pools, collateral agreements, or arrangements with liquidity providers are
employed, ensuring ample funds for settlement. Furthermore, posting only occurs after settlement.
Technology risks present in Faster Payments
Technology risk refers to any potential for technology failures to disrupt business. There are a number of
different types of risks that can expose the vulnerabilities of the technology of Faster Payments systems.
1. External attacks, most likely cyber-attacks rather than physical attacks on the system’s premises,
can cause significant issues related to the loss or theft of sensitive data, damage in the operating
technologies, and/or the integrity of the system.
2. Mistakes in the programming can result in operational errors and thus unplanned downtime when
system updates take place.
3. While the modern payments landscape is more and more automated, there is still room for human
error in the process of building and maintaining information technology systems.
4. Technology systems run on electricity and are vulnerable to outages.
5. While most Faster Payments systems have modern infrastructures, there is a theoretical possibility
that they reach their processing limits and overload, and therefore would not be able to fulfill their
responsibilities related to the speed of processing.
6. System owners often outsource their infrastructure operations to technology service providers, thus
all the above-mentioned risks can also affect the system itself even if they are not directly connected
to the system owner.
Credit risks present in Faster Payments
Credit risk is the risk that a party to a transaction will not be able to provide the necessary funds, as contracted,
for settlement to take place on the scheduled date (i.e., it arises when a party will not settle an obligation for full
value). Each Faster Payment instrument has a specific settlement process that depends on the entities involved.

THE 2026 AFPP HANDBOOK

109

C H A P T ER THR EE – FASTER PAYMENTS RISK MAN AG EMEN T

As it relates to Same Day ACH processing, both ODFIs and RDFIs are exposed to credit risk; however,
ODFIs have the greatest credit risk. Their losses generally occur due to failure or bankruptcy by their
Originator. ODFIs are exposed to different levels of credit risk for Same Day ACH credit and debit entries.
An ODFI’s credit risk associated with Same Day ACH credit entries is the period of time between when the
file is initiated to the time the Originator funds their account for settlement. For example, if a Same Day
ACH credit transaction is transmitted to the ACH Operator by the 10:30 AM ET processing deadline, it
will not settle until 1 PM ET. Thus, the ODFI’s credit exposure would be a few hours.
For Same Day ACH debit entries, credit risk is from the time the funds are made available to the Originator
to the time entries cannot be returned by the RDFI. For creditworthy Originators, it is customary for
the ODFI to provide the Originator with availability of funds for the total amount of the debit file on
Settlement Date. The ODFI of a debit file is exposed to the risk that entries may be returned by the RDFI
and that it may be unable to recover the amount of those funds previously released to the Originator. The
shorter the time in which an RDFI must return an entry, the lower the risk to the ODFI that the Originator
will have gone out of business or have insufficient funds in the account to cover the chargeback of the debit.
So, the ODFI’s credit risk can range from 2 banking days (items that cannot be posted) to 60 calendar days
(disputed ACH debit entries). And since ACH debits are generally sent on a recurring basis, the credit risk
associated with them can compound making ACH debits a higher risk than credit entries.
An RDFI’s potential credit risk and loss occurs when it (1) posts a Same Day ACH credit entry and makes
funds available for withdrawal prior to receiving final settlement, or (2) mishandles the return of a Same
Day ACH debit entry (e.g., returns the debit outside of the prescribed timeframes or uses an incorrect
return reason code).
Much like an ODFI in the ACH Network, a provider of RTP or FedNow origination services could experience
credit risk or a loss due to failure or bankruptcy by the entity initiating the payment. Of course, they can easily
manage this by using a “good funds model.” That is, ensure they debit the Sender’s account prior to transmitting
the payment into the network. But they may decide for some business clients, they are willing to allow an intraday
overdraft and will transmit the payment. However, if the business client’s cash position remains overdrawn, then
they could suffer a financial loss.
There is no credit risk associated with receiving an RTP or FedNow credit payment as both networks require
funds be made available to the account holder by the receiving institution upon receipt of a Message Status
Report. The Message Status Report is sent after settlement has been processed to the receiving institution.

Types and application of internal controls (user access controls) related
to Faster Payments
Evolving technology and the increased volume of payment transactions have made fraud increasingly more
relevant. Faster Payments must uphold internal controls encompassing administrative controls and internal
accounting controls, which cover account types and limits, access controls, the segregation of duties, and the
reconcilement of input to output as well as management review. Faster Payments use various fraud detection
and mitigation techniques to ensure a safe experience for consumers. Though the implementation of internal
controls has its own barriers and limitations, diverse methodologies and techniques on risk assessment as well
as various requirements guarantee a secure and undisturbed transaction journey.

110

C H A P T ER THR EE – FASTER PAYMENTS RISK MAN AG EMEN T

Application of fraud detection and mitigation techniques for different types of fraud
As described in the “Fraud classifications” section of this handbook, fraud can be generally classified into
authorized and unauthorized fraud. Accordingly, there are specific fraud detection and mitigation techniques that are particularly appropriate for each of these fraud types.
An example of authorized fraud is where fraudsters manipulate the account holder to make a payment to the
fraudster by tricking them into believing the fraudster is someone they trust. Combatting authorized fraud
requires focus on mitigation techniques such as consumer education and fraud awareness in addition to using
account validation tools such as Confirmation-of-Payee (COP), an account name verification service designed
to reduce fraud and misdirected payments by ensuring that the name entered by a payer matches the name
on the recipient’s account. It has become a key fraud prevention tool in the payments industry, particularly in
combating Authorized Push Payment (APP) fraud.
Looking at unauthorized fraud, fraudsters use sophisticated techniques to take over accounts or initiate
fraudulent payments by misusing account information. Strong account holder authentication is a key preventative
tool along with fraud monitoring systems. Authorized Push Payment (APP) fraud is a growing threat in the
payments industry, particularly as real-time and faster payment systems become more widespread. APP fraud
occurs when a consumer is tricked into authorizing a payment to a fraudster—often under false pretenses such
as investment scams, romance scams, or impersonation of trusted individuals
The table provided below offers a non-exhaustive breakdown of mitigation and detection techniques tailored
to the distinct characteristics and challenges of each type of fraud.
